<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Glaucoma</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<p><b>Glaucoma</b> is a group of eye diseases which result in damage to the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_loss" title="Vision loss">vision loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> The most common type is open-angle glaucoma with less common types including closed-angle glaucoma and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal-tension_glaucoma" title="Normal-tension glaucoma">normal-tension glaucoma</a>. Open-angle glaucoma develops slowly over time and there is no pain. <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">Side vision</a> may begin to decrease followed by central vision resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">blindness</a> if not treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> Closed-angle glaucoma can present gradually or suddenly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> The sudden presentation may involve severe eye pain, blurred vision, mid-dilated <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a>, redness of the eye, and nausea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Vision loss from glaucoma, once it has occurred, is permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Risk factors for glaucoma include increased <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">pressure in the eye</a>, a family history of the condition, <a href="/wiki/Migraine" title="Migraine">migraines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">high blood pressure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> For eye pressures a value of greater than 21 mmHg or 2.8 kPa is often used with higher pressures leading to a greater risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> However, some may have high eye pressure for years and never develop damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Conversely, optic nerve damage may occur with normal pressure, known as normal-tension glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The mechanism of open-angle glaucoma is believed to be slow exit of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueous_humor" title="Aqueous humor">aqueous humor</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a> while in closed-angle glaucoma the <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a> blocks the trabecular meshwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Diagnosis is by a dilated <a href="/wiki/Eye_examination" title="Eye examination">eye examination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> Often the optic nerve shows an abnormal amount of <a href="/wiki/Optic_cup_(anatomical)" title="Optic cup (anatomical)">cupping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>If treated early it is possible to slow or stop the progression of disease with medication, <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> treatment, or surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> The goal of these treatments is to decrease eye pressure. A number of different classes of <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_medication" title="Glaucoma medication">glaucoma medication</a> are available. Laser treatments may be effective in both open-angle and closed-angle glaucoma. A number of types of <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_surgery" title="Glaucoma surgery">glaucoma surgeries</a> may be used in people who do not respond sufficiently to other measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Treatment of closed-angle glaucoma is a medical emergency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 11 to 67 million people have glaucoma globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The disease affects about 2 million people in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> It occurs more commonly among older people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2016Fact_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2016Fact-1">[1]</a></sup> Closed-angle glaucoma is more common in women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Glaucoma has been called the "silent thief of sight" because the loss of vision usually occurs slowly over a long period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Worldwide, glaucoma is the second-leading cause of blindness after <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The word "glaucoma" is from ancient Greek <i>glaukos</i> which means blue, green, or gray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lef2015_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lef2015-8">[8]</a></sup> In English, the word was used as early as 1587 but did not become commonly used until after 1850, when the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmoscope" title="Ophthalmoscope">ophthalmoscope</a> allowed people to see the optic nerve damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Primary_glaucoma_and_its_variants"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Primary glaucoma and its variants</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="360" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg/220px-Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg/330px-Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acute_Angle_Closure-glaucoma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photo showing conjunctival vessels dilated at the corneal edge (ciliary flush, circumcorneal flush) and hazy cornea characteristic of acute angle closure glaucoma</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Open-angle glaucoma is painless and does not have acute attacks, thus the lack of clear symptoms make screening via regular eye check-ups important. The only signs are gradually progressive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_field_loss" title="Visual field loss">visual field loss</a>, and optic nerve changes (increased <a href="/wiki/Cup-to-disc_ratio" title="Cup-to-disc ratio">cup-to-disc ratio</a> on <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmoscopy" title="Ophthalmoscopy">fundoscopic examination</a>).</p>
<p>About 10% of people with closed angles present with acute angle closure characterized by sudden ocular pain, seeing halos around lights, red eye, very high intraocular pressure (&gt;30 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MmHg" title="MmHg">mmHg</a>), nausea and vomiting, suddenly decreased vision, and a fixed, mid-dilated pupil. It is also associated with an oval pupil in some cases. Acute angle closure is an emergency.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eye_disease_simulation,_normal_vision.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1800" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg/220px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg/330px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg/440px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_normal_vision.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eye_disease_simulation,_normal_vision.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A normal range of vision</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eye_disease_simulation,_glaucoma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg/220px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg/330px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg/440px-Eye_disease_simulation%2C_glaucoma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eye_disease_simulation,_glaucoma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The same view with advanced vision loss from glaucoma</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Of the several causes for glaucoma, ocular hypertension (increased pressure within the eye) is the most important risk factor in most glaucomas, but in some populations, only 50% of people with primary open-angle glaucoma actually have elevated ocular pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sommer1991_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sommer1991-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Open-angle glaucoma accounts for 90% of glaucoma cases in the United States. Closed-angle glaucoma accounts for less than 10% of glaucoma cases in the United States, but as many as half of glaucoma cases in other nations (particularly East Asian countries).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary">Dietary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Dietary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>No clear evidence indicates vitamin deficiencies cause glaucoma in humans. It follows, then, that oral vitamin supplementation is not a recommended treatment for glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhee2001_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee2001-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">Caffeine</a> increases <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">intraocular pressure</a> in those with glaucoma, but does not appear to affect normal individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnicity">Ethnicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ethnicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many people of <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asian</a> descent are prone to developing angle closure glaucoma due to shallower anterior chamber depths, with the majority of cases of glaucoma in this population consisting of some form of angle closure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2002_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2002-13">[13]</a></sup> Higher rates of glaucoma have also been reported for <a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a> populations, compared to white populations, in Canada and Greenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedman_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedman-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Positive family history is a risk factor for glaucoma. The relative risk of having primary open-angle glaucoma (P.O.A.G.) is increased about two- to four-fold for people who have a sibling with glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yanoff_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-yanoff-15">[15]</a></sup> Glaucoma, particularly primary open-angle glaucoma, is associated with <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> in several <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MYOC" title="MYOC">MYOC</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ASB10&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ASB10 (page does not exist)">ASB10</a>, <a href="/wiki/WDR36" title="WDR36">WDR36</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neurotrophin-4" title="Neurotrophin-4">NTF4</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/TANK-binding_kinase_1" title="TANK-binding kinase 1">TBK1</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMIM137760_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-OMIM137760-16">[16]</a></sup> although most cases of glaucoma do not involve these genetic mutations. Normal-tension glaucoma, which comprises one-third of POAG, is also associated with genetic mutations (including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OPA1" title="OPA1">OPA1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Optineurin" title="Optineurin">OPTN</a></i> genes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Various rare congenital/genetic eye malformations are associated with glaucoma. Occasionally, failure of the normal third-trimester gestational atrophy of the <a href="/wiki/Hyaloid_canal" title="Hyaloid canal">hyaloid canal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Persistent_tunica_vasculosa_lentis" title="Persistent tunica vasculosa lentis">tunica vasculosa lentis</a> is associated with other anomalies. Angle closure-induced <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">ocular hypertension</a> and glaucomatous optic neuropathy may also occur with these anomalies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pardianto2005_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pardianto2005-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaum2004_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaum2004-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hunt2005_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hunt2005-20">[20]</a></sup> and has been modelled in mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2001_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2001-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other factors can cause glaucoma, known as "secondary glaucoma", including prolonged use of <a href="/wiki/Anabolic_steroid" title="Anabolic steroid">steroids</a> (steroid-induced glaucoma); conditions that severely restrict blood flow to the eye, such as severe <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">diabetic retinopathy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_retinal_vein_occlusion" title="Central retinal vein occlusion">central retinal vein occlusion</a> (neovascular glaucoma); <a href="/wiki/Blast-related_ocular_trauma" title="Blast-related ocular trauma">ocular trauma</a> (angle-recession glaucoma); and inflammation of the middle layer of the pigmented vascular eye structure (<a href="/wiki/Uveitis" title="Uveitis">uveitis</a>), known as uveitic glaucoma.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="350" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png/220px-Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png/330px-Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Human_eye_cross-sectional_view_grayscale.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Human eye cross-sectional view</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The underlying cause of open-angle glaucoma remains unclear. Several theories exist on its exact etiology. However, the major risk factor for most glaucomas and the focus of treatment is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Increased_intraocular_pressure" title="Increased intraocular pressure">increased intraocular pressure</a>. Intraocular pressure is a function of production of liquid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueous_humor" title="Aqueous humor">aqueous humor</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_processes" title="Ciliary processes">ciliary processes</a> of the eye, and its drainage through the <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a>. Aqueous humor flows from the ciliary processes into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posterior_chamber" title="Posterior chamber">posterior chamber</a>, bounded posteriorly by the <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zonules_of_Zinn" title="Zonules of Zinn">zonules of Zinn</a>, and anteriorly by the <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a>. It then flows through the <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a> of the iris into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_chamber" title="Anterior chamber">anterior chamber</a>, bounded posteriorly by the iris and anteriorly by the <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a>. From here, the trabecular meshwork drains aqueous humor via the scleral venous sinus (<a href="/wiki/Schlemm%27s_canal" title="Schlemm's canal">Schlemm's canal</a>) into <a href="/wiki/Intrascleral_plexus" title="Intrascleral plexus">scleral plexuses</a> and general blood circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alguire_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alguire-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In open/wide-angle glaucoma, flow is reduced through the trabecular meshwork, due to the degeneration and obstruction of the trabecular meshwork, whose original function is to absorb the aqueous humor. Loss of aqueous humor absorption leads to increased resistance and thus a chronic, painless buildup of pressure in the eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mozaffarieh2008_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mozaffarieh2008-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In close/narrow-angle, the iridocorneal angle is completely closed because of forward displacement of the final roll and root of the iris against the cornea, resulting in the inability of the aqueous fluid to flow from the posterior to the anterior chamber and then out of the trabecular network. This accumulation of aqueous humor causes an acute increase in pressure and pain.</p>
<p>The inconsistent relationship of glaucomatous optic neuropathy with increased intraocular pressure has provoked hypotheses and studies on anatomic structure, eye development, nerve compression trauma, optic nerve blood flow, excitatory neurotransmitter, trophic factor, retinal ganglion cell/axon degeneration, glial support cell, immune system, aging mechanisms of neuron loss, and severing of the nerve fibers at the scleral edge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin2008_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin2008-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Varma2008_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Varma2008-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hernandez2008_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hernandez2008-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cantor2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor2006-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz2007_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz2007-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morrison2006_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morrison2006-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knox2007_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knox2007-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tezel2007_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tezel2007-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry2006_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berry2006-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CanJOphth42-3-3_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-CanJOphth42-3-3-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Screening for glaucoma is usually performed as part of a standard <a href="/wiki/Eye_examination" title="Eye examination">eye examination</a> performed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optometrist" title="Optometrist">optometrists</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmologist" title="Ophthalmologist">ophthalmologists</a>. Testing for glaucoma should include measurements of the intraocular pressure via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonometry" title="Tonometry">tonometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> anterior chamber angle examination or <a href="/wiki/Gonioscopy" title="Gonioscopy">gonioscopy</a>, and examination of the optic nerve to look for any visible damage to it, or change in the <a href="/wiki/Cup-to-disc_ratio" title="Cup-to-disc ratio">cup-to-disc ratio</a> and also rim appearance and vascular change. A formal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perimetry" title="Perimetry">visual field test</a> should be performed. The retinal nerve fiber layer can be assessed with imaging techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Optical_coherence_tomography" title="Optical coherence tomography">optical coherence tomography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scanning_laser_polarimetry" title="Scanning laser polarimetry">scanning laser polarimetry</a>, and/or <a href="/wiki/Scanning_laser_ophthalmoscopy" title="Scanning laser ophthalmoscopy">scanning laser ophthalmoscopy</a> (Heidelberg retinal tomogram).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pardianto2006_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pardianto2006-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2006_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2006-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michelessi_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michelessi-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Owing to the sensitivity of all methods of tonometry to corneal thickness, methods such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonometry" title="Tonometry">Goldmann tonometry</a> should be augmented with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pachymetry" title="Pachymetry">pachymetry</a> to measure the central corneal thickness (CCT). A thicker-than-average cornea can result in a pressure reading higher than the 'true' pressure whereas a thinner-than-average cornea can produce a pressure reading lower than the 'true' pressure.</p>
<p>Because pressure measurement error can be caused by more than just CCT (i.e., corneal hydration, elastic properties, etc.), it is impossible to 'adjust' pressure measurements based only on CCT measurements. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_Doubling_Illusion" title="Frequency Doubling Illusion">frequency doubling illusion</a> can also be used to detect glaucoma with the use of a frequency doubling technology perimeter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson2003_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2003-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Examination for glaucoma also could be assessed with more attention given to sex, race, history of drug use, refraction, inheritance and family history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pardianto2006_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pardianto2006-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Glaucoma tests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glaucoma.org_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-glaucoma.org-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vision.about.com_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-vision.about.com-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glaucomaassociates.com_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-glaucomaassociates.com-43">[43]</a></sup></caption>
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>What test examines</th>
<th>How it is accomplished</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonometry" title="Tonometry">Tonometry</a></td>
<td>Inner eye pressure</td>
<td>The eye is numbed via eye drops. The examiner then uses a tonometer to measure the inner pressure of the eye through pressure applied by a puff of warm air or a tiny tool.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ophthalmoscopy" title="Ophthalmoscopy">Ophthalmoscopy</a> (dilated eye examination)</td>
<td>Shape and color of the optic nerve</td>
<td>The pupil is dilated via the application of eye drops. Using a small magnification device with a light on the end, the examiner can examine the magnified optic nerve.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perimetry" title="Perimetry">Perimetry</a> (visual field test)</td>
<td>Complete field of vision</td>
<td>The patient looks straight ahead and is asked to indicate when light passes the patient's peripheral field of vision. This allows the examiner to map the patient’s field of vision.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Gonioscopy" title="Gonioscopy">Gonioscopy</a></td>
<td>Angle in the eye where the iris meets the cornea</td>
<td>Eyedrops are used to numb the eye. A hand-held contact lens with a mirror is placed gently on the eye to allow the examiner to see the angle between the cornea and the iris.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pachymetry" title="Pachymetry">Pachymetry</a></td>
<td>Thickness of the cornea</td>
<td>The examiner places a pachymeter gently on the front of the eye to measure its thickness.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDx" title="GDx">Nerve fiber analysis</a></td>
<td>Thickness of the nerve fiber layer</td>
<td>Using one of several techniques, the nerve fibers are examined.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Glaucoma has been classified into specific types:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paton_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paton-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_glaucoma_and_its_variants">Primary glaucoma and its variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Primary glaucoma and its variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Primary glaucoma (H40.1-H40.2)</p>
<ul>
<li>Primary open-angle glaucoma, also known as chronic open-angle glaucoma, chronic simple glaucoma, glaucoma simplex</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>High-tension glaucoma</li>
<li>Low-tension glaucoma</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Primary angle closure glaucoma, also known as primary closed-angle glaucoma, narrow-angle glaucoma, pupil-block glaucoma, acute congestive glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Acute angle closure glaucoma (<i>aka</i> AACG)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loganabbrev_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-loganabbrev-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>Chronic angle closure glaucoma</li>
<li>Intermittent angle closure glaucoma</li>
<li>Superimposed on chronic open-angle closure glaucoma ("combined mechanism" – uncommon)</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Variants of primary glaucoma</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pigment_dispersion_syndrome" title="Pigment dispersion syndrome">Pigmentary glaucoma</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exfoliation_glaucoma" title="Exfoliation glaucoma">Exfoliation glaucoma</a>, also known as pseudoexfoliative glaucoma or glaucoma capsulare</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Primary_juvenile_glaucoma" title="Primary juvenile glaucoma">Primary juvenile glaucoma</a></li>
</ul>
<p><b>Primary angle closure glaucoma</b> is caused by contact between the iris and trabecular meshwork, which in turn obstructs outflow of the aqueous humor from the eye. This contact between iris and <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a> (TM) may gradually damage the function of the meshwork until it fails to keep pace with aqueous production, and the pressure rises. In over half of all cases, prolonged contact between iris and TM causes the formation of synechiae (effectively "scars").</p>
<p>These cause permanent obstruction of aqueous outflow. In some cases, pressure may rapidly build up in the eye, causing pain and redness (symptomatic, or so-called "acute" angle closure). In this situation, the vision may become blurred, and halos may be seen around bright lights. Accompanying symptoms may include a headache and vomiting.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made from physical signs and symptoms: pupils mid-dilated and unresponsive to light, cornea edematous (cloudy), reduced vision, redness, and pain. However, the majority of cases are asymptomatic. Prior to the very severe loss of vision, these cases can only be identified by examination, generally by an eye care professional.</p>
<p>Once any symptoms have been controlled, the first line (and often definitive) treatment is laser <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iridotomy" title="Iridotomy">iridotomy</a>. This may be performed using either Nd:YAG or argon lasers, or in some cases by conventional incisional surgery. The goal of treatment is to reverse and prevent, contact between the iris and trabecular meshwork. In early to moderately advanced cases, iridotomy is successful in opening the angle in around 75% of cases. In the other 25%, laser iridoplasty, medication (pilocarpine) or incisional surgery may be required.</p>
<p><b>Primary open-angle glaucoma</b> is when optic nerve damage results in a progressive loss of the visual field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck-poag_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-poag-46">[46]</a></sup> This is associated with increased pressure in the eye. Not all people with primary open-angle glaucoma have eye pressure that is elevated beyond normal, but decreasing the eye pressure further has been shown to stop progression even in these cases.</p>
<p>The increased pressure is caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trabecular" title="Trabecular">trabecular</a> blockage. Because the microscopic passageways are blocked, the pressure builds up in the eye and causes imperceptible very gradual vision loss. Peripheral vision is affected first, but eventually the entire vision will be lost if not treated.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made by looking for cupping of the optic nerve. Prostaglandin agonists work by opening uveoscleral passageways. Beta-blockers, such as timolol, work by decreasing aqueous formation. Carbonic anhydrase inhibitors decrease bicarbonate formation from ciliary processes in the eye, thus decreasing the formation of Aqueous humor. Parasympathetic analogs are drugs that work on the trabecular outflow by opening up the passageway and constricting the pupil. Alpha 2 agonists (<a href="/wiki/Brimonidine" title="Brimonidine">brimonidine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apraclonidine" title="Apraclonidine">apraclonidine</a>) both decrease fluid production (via. inhibition of AC) and increase drainage.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental_glaucoma">Developmental glaucoma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Developmental glaucoma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Developmental glaucoma (Q15.0)</p>
<ul>
<li>Primary congenital glaucoma</li>
<li>Infantile glaucoma</li>
<li>Glaucoma associated with hereditary or familial diseases</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary_glaucoma">Secondary glaucoma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Secondary glaucoma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Secondary glaucoma (H40.3-H40.6)</p>
<ul>
<li>Inflammatory glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Uveitis of all types</li>
<li>Fuchs heterochromic iridocyclitis</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Phacogenic glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Angle-closure glaucoma with mature cataract</li>
<li>Phacoanaphylactic glaucoma secondary to rupture of lens capsule</li>
<li>Phacolytic glaucoma due to phacotoxic meshwork blockage</li>
<li>Subluxation of lens</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Glaucoma secondary to intraocular hemorrhage</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Hyphema</li>
<li>Hemolytic glaucoma, also known as erythroclastic glaucoma</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Traumatic glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Angle recession glaucoma: Traumatic recession on anterior chamber angle</li>
<li>Postsurgical glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Aphakic pupillary block</li>
<li>Ciliary block glaucoma</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Neovascular glaucoma (see below for more details)</li>
<li>Drug-induced glaucoma</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Corticosteroid induced glaucoma</li>
<li>Alpha-chymotrypsin glaucoma. Postoperative ocular hypertension from use of alpha chymotrypsin.</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Glaucoma of miscellaneous origin</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Associated with intraocular tumors</li>
<li>Associated with retinal detachments</li>
<li>Secondary to severe chemical burns of the eye</li>
<li>Associated with essential iris atrophy</li>
<li>Toxic glaucoma</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p><b>Neovascular glaucoma</b>, an uncommon type of glaucoma, is difficult or nearly impossible to treat, and is often caused by proliferative <a href="/wiki/Diabetic_retinopathy" title="Diabetic retinopathy">diabetic retinopathy</a> (PDR) or <a href="/wiki/Central_retinal_vein_occlusion" title="Central retinal vein occlusion">central retinal vein occlusion</a> (CRVO). It may also be triggered by other conditions that result in <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">ischemia</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_body" title="Ciliary body">ciliary body</a>. Individuals with poor blood flow to the eye are highly at risk for this condition.</p>
<p>Neovascular glaucoma results when new, abnormal vessels begin developing in the angle of the eye that begin blocking the drainage. Patients with such condition begin to rapidly lose their eyesight. Sometimes, the disease appears very rapidly, especially after cataract surgery procedures. A new treatment for this disease, as first reported by Kahook and colleagues, involves the use of a novel group of medications known as <a href="/wiki/Anti%E2%80%93vascular_endothelial_growth_factor_therapy" title="Anti–vascular endothelial growth factor therapy">anti-VEGF agents</a>. These injectable medications can lead to a dramatic decrease in new vessel formation and, if injected early enough in the disease process, may lead to normalization of intraocular pressure. Currently, there are no high-quality controlled trials demonstrating a beneficial effect of anti-VEGF treatments in lowering IOP in people with neovascular glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simha_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simha-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><b>Toxic glaucoma</b> is open angle glaucoma with an unexplained significant rise of <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">intraocular pressure</a> following unknown pathogenesis. Intraocular pressure can sometimes reach 80 mmHg (11 kPa). It characteristically manifests as <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_body" title="Ciliary body">ciliary body</a> inflammation and massive trabecular o<a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a> that sometimes extends to <a href="/wiki/Schlemm%27s_canal" title="Schlemm's canal">Schlemm's canal</a>. This condition is differentiated from malignant glaucoma by the presence of a deep and clear anterior chamber and a lack of aqueous misdirection. Also, the corneal appearance is not as hazy. A reduction in visual acuity can occur followed neuroretinal breakdown.</p>
<p>Associated factors include inflammation, drugs, trauma and intraocular surgery, including cataract surgery and vitrectomy procedures. Gede Pardianto (2005) reported on four patients who had toxic glaucoma. One of them underwent phacoemulsification with small particle nucleus drops. Some cases can be resolved with some medication, vitrectomy procedures or trabeculectomy. Valving procedures can give some relief, but further research is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absolute_glaucoma">Absolute glaucoma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Absolute glaucoma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Absolute glaucoma (H44.5) is the end stage of all types of glaucoma. The eye has no vision, absence of <a href="/wiki/Pupillary_light_reflex" title="Pupillary light reflex">pupillary light reflex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pupillary_response" title="Pupillary response">pupillary response</a>, and has a stony appearance. Severe pain is present in the eye. The treatment of absolute glaucoma is a destructive procedure like cyclocryoapplication, cyclophotocoagulation, or injection of 99% alcohol.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(August 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Glaucoma is an umbrella term for eye conditions which damage the <a href="/wiki/Optic_nerve" title="Optic nerve">optic nerve</a>, and which can lead to a loss of vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sit2006_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sit2006-49">[49]</a></sup> The main cause of damage to the optic nerve is <a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">intraocular pressure</a> (IOP), excessive fluid pressure within the eye, which can be due to various reasons including blockage of drainage ducts, and narrowing or closure of the angle between the <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a> and cornea.</p>
<p>The primary division in categorizing different types of glaucoma is open-angle and closed-angle (or angle-closure) glaucoma. The open angle refers to the angle where the iris meets the cornea being as wide and open as it should be, allowing the fluid from inside the eye to drain, thus relieving the internal pressure. Where this angle is narrowed or closed, pressure can build up, and eventually damage the optic nerve leading to loss of vision.</p>
<p>Primary open-angle glaucoma (also, <i>primary glaucoma</i>, <i>chronic glaucoma</i>) refers to slow clogging of the drainage canals resulting in increased eye pressure which causes progressive optic nerve damage. This manifests as a gradual loss of the visual field, starting with a loss of <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_vision" title="Peripheral vision">peripheral vision</a>, but eventually the entire vision will be lost if not treated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merck-poag_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merck-poag-46">[46]</a></sup> This is the most common type of glaucoma, accounting for 90% of cases in the United States, but fewer in Asian countries. Onset is slow and painless, and loss of vision is gradual and irreversible.</p>
<p>Narrow-angle glaucoma (also <i>closed-angle glaucoma</i>) the iris bows forward, narrowing the angle that drains the eye, increasing pressure within the eye. If untreated, it can lead to the medical emergency of angle-closure glaucoma.</p>
<p>In angle-closure glaucoma (also <i>closed-angle glaucoma</i>, <i>primary angle-closure glaucoma</i>, <i>acute glaucoma</i>) the iris bows forward and causes physical contact between the iris and <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a>, which blocks the outflow of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueous_humor" title="Aqueous humor">aqueous humor</a> from within the eye. This contact may gradually damage the draining function of the meshwork until it fails to keep pace with aqueous production, and the intraocular pressure rises. The onset of symptoms is sudden and causes pain and other symptoms that are noticeable; it is treated as a medical emergency. Unlike open-angle glaucoma, angle-closure glaucoma is a result of the angle between the iris and cornea closing. This tends to occur in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far-sighted" title="Far-sighted">far-sighted</a>, who have smaller-than-normal anterior chambers, making physical contact between the iris and trabecular meshwork more likely.</p>
<p>Normal-tension glaucoma (also <i>NTG</i>, <i>low-tension glaucoma</i>, <i>normal-pressure glaucoma</i>) is a condition where the optic nerve is damaged although intraocular pressure (IOP) is in the normal range (12-22mm Hg). Individuals with a family history of NTG, those of Japanese ancestry, and those with a history of systemic heart disease are at higher than average risk of developing NTG. The cause of NTG is unknown.</p>
<p>Secondary glaucoma refers to any case in which another disease, trauma, drug or procedure causes increased eye pressure, resulting in optic nerve damage and vision loss, and may be mild or severe. It can be due to an eye injury, inflammation, a tumor, or advanced cases of cataracts or diabetes. It can also be caused by certain drugs such as steroids. Treatment depends on whether it is open-angle or angle-closure glaucoma.</p>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudoexfoliation_glaucoma" title="Pseudoexfoliation glaucoma">pseudoexfoliation glaucoma</a> (also, <i>PEX</i>, <i>exfoliation glaucoma</i>) the pressure is due to the accumulation of microscopic granular protein fibers, which can block normal drainage of the aqueous humor. PEX is prevalent in Scandinavia, primarily in those over 70, and more commonly in women.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigmentary_glaucoma" title="Pigmentary glaucoma">Pigmentary glaucoma</a> (also, pigmentary dispersion syndrome) is caused by pigment cells sloughing off from the back of the iris and floating around in the aqueous humor. Over time, these pigment cells can accumulate in the anterior chamber in such a way that it can begin to clog the trabecular meshwork. It is a rare condition that occurs mostly among Caucasians, mostly males in their mid-20s to 40s, and most are nearsighted.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Primary_juvenile_glaucoma" title="Primary juvenile glaucoma">Primary juvenile glaucoma</a> is a neonate or juvenile abnormality where ocular hypertension is evident at birth or shortly thereafter and is caused by abnormalities in the anterior chamber angle development that blocks the outflow of the aqueous humor.</p>
<p>Uveitic Glaucoma is due to uveitis, the swelling and inflammation of the <a href="/wiki/Uvea" title="Uvea">uvea</a>, the middle layer of the eye. The uvea provides most of the blood supply to the retina. Increased eye pressure in uveitis can result from the inflammation itself or from the steroids used to treat it.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Screening">Screening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Screening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Preventive_Services_Task_Force" title="United States Preventive Services Task Force">United States Preventive Services Task Force</a> as of 2013 states there is insufficient evidence to recommend for or against screening for glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Therefore, there is no national screening program in the US. Screening, however, is recommended starting at age 40 by the American Academy of Ophthalmology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a glaucoma screening program in the UK. Those at risk are advised to have a <a href="/wiki/Dilated_fundus_examination" title="Dilated fundus examination">dilated eye examination</a> at least once a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p><span id="Management"></span></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The modern goals of glaucoma management are to avoid glaucomatous damage and nerve damage, and preserve visual field and total quality of life for patients, with minimal side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Necker2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Necker2006-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parikh2007_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parikh2007-53">[53]</a></sup> This requires appropriate diagnostic techniques and follow-up examinations, and judicious selection of treatments for the individual patient. Although intraocular pressure is only one of the major risk factors for glaucoma, lowering it via various pharmaceuticals and/or surgical techniques is currently the mainstay of glaucoma treatment.</p>
<p>Vascular flow and neurodegenerative theories of glaucomatous optic neuropathy have prompted studies on various neuroprotective therapeutic strategies, including nutritional compounds, some of which may be regarded by clinicians as safe for use now, while others are on trial.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication">Medication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latanoprost.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2313" data-file-width="3085" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Latanoprost.jpg/220px-Latanoprost.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Latanoprost.jpg/330px-Latanoprost.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Latanoprost.jpg/440px-Latanoprost.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latanoprost.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Latanoprost</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_medication" title="Glaucoma medication">Glaucoma medication</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Intraocular_pressure" title="Intraocular pressure">Intraocular pressure</a> can be lowered with medication, usually eye drops. Several classes of medications are used to treat glaucoma, with several medications in each class.</p>
<p>Each of these medicines may have local and systemic side effects. Adherence to medication protocol can be confusing and expensive; if side effects occur, the patient must be willing either to tolerate them or to communicate with the treating physician to improve the drug regimen. Initially, glaucoma drops may reasonably be started in either one or in both eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMC_Ophthalmol_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMC_Ophthalmol-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Poor compliance with medications and follow-up visits is a major reason for vision loss in glaucoma patients. A 2003 study of patients in an <a href="/wiki/Health_maintenance_organization" title="Health maintenance organization">HMO</a> found half failed to fill their prescriptions the first time, and one-fourth failed to refill their prescriptions a second time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Patient education and communication must be ongoing to sustain successful treatment plans for this lifelong disease with no early symptoms.</p>
<p>The possible neuroprotective effects of various topical and systemic medications are also being investigated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhee2001_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rhee2001-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritch2007_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritch2007-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsai_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsai-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mozaffarieh_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mozaffarieh-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostaglandin_analogs" title="Prostaglandin analogs">Prostaglandin analogs</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Latanoprost" title="Latanoprost">latanoprost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bimatoprost" title="Bimatoprost">bimatoprost</a> and <a href="/wiki/Travoprost" title="Travoprost">travoprost</a>, increase uveoscleral outflow of aqueous humor. Bimatoprost also increases trabecular outflow.</li>
<li>Topical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-adrenergic_receptor_antagonists" title="Beta-adrenergic receptor antagonists">beta-adrenergic receptor antagonists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Timolol" title="Timolol">timolol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levobunolol" title="Levobunolol">levobunolol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Betaxolol" title="Betaxolol">betaxolol</a>, decrease aqueous humor production by the epithelium of the <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_body" title="Ciliary body">ciliary body</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha2-adrenergic_agonist" title="Alpha2-adrenergic agonist">Alpha2-adrenergic agonists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Brimonidine" title="Brimonidine">brimonidine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apraclonidine" title="Apraclonidine">apraclonidine</a>, work by a dual mechanism, decreasing aqueous humor production and increasing uveoscleral outflow.</li>
<li>Less-selective <a href="/wiki/Alpha-adrenergic_agonist" title="Alpha-adrenergic agonist">alpha agonists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine" title="Epinephrine">epinephrine</a>, decrease aqueous humor production through vasoconstriction of ciliary body blood vessels, useful only in open-angle glaucoma. Epinephrine's mydriatic effect, however, renders it unsuitable for closed-angle glaucoma due to further narrowing of the uveoscleral outflow (i.e. further closure of trabecular meshwork, which is responsible for absorption of aqueous humor).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Miosis" title="Miosis">Miotic agents</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasympathomimetic" title="Parasympathomimetic">parasympathomimetics</a>), such as <a href="/wiki/Pilocarpine" title="Pilocarpine">pilocarpine</a>, work by contraction of the <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_muscle" title="Ciliary muscle">ciliary muscle</a>, opening the <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a> and allowing increased outflow of the aqueous humour. <a href="/wiki/Echothiophate" title="Echothiophate">Echothiophate</a>, an acetylcholinesterase inhibitor, is used in chronic glaucoma.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase_inhibitors" title="Carbonic anhydrase inhibitors">Carbonic anhydrase inhibitors</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Dorzolamide" title="Dorzolamide">dorzolamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brinzolamide" title="Brinzolamide">brinzolamide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Acetazolamide" title="Acetazolamide">acetazolamide</a>, lower secretion of aqueous humor by inhibiting carbonic anhydrase in the ciliary body.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laser">Laser</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Laser">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Argon laser trabeculoplasty (ALT) may be used to treat open-angle glaucoma, but this is a temporary solution, not a cure. A 50-μm argon laser spot is aimed at the trabecular meshwork to stimulate the opening of the mesh to allow more outflow of aqueous fluid. Usually, half of the angle is treated at a time. Traditional laser trabeculoplasty uses a thermal argon laser in an <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> laser trabeculoplasty procedure.</p>
<p>A newer type of laser trabeculoplasty uses a "cold" (nonthermal) laser to stimulate drainage in the trabecular meshwork. This newer procedure, selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT), uses a 532-nm, frequency-doubled, Q-switched <a href="/wiki/Nd:YAG_laser" title="Nd:YAG laser">Nd:YAG laser</a>, which selectively targets <a href="/wiki/Melanin" title="Melanin">melanin</a> pigment in the trabecular meshwork cells. Studies show SLT is as effective as ALT at lowering eye pressure. In addition, SLT may be repeated three to four times, whereas ALT can usually be repeated only once.</p>
<p>Nd:YAG laser peripheral iridotomy (LPI) may be used in patients susceptible to or affected by angle closure glaucoma or <a href="/wiki/Pigment_dispersion_syndrome" title="Pigment dispersion syndrome">pigment dispersion syndrome</a>. During laser iridotomy, laser energy is used to make a small, full-thickness opening in the iris to equalize the pressure between the front and back of the iris, thus correcting any abnormal bulging of the iris. In people with narrow angles, this can uncover the trabecular meshwork. In some cases of intermittent or short-term angle closure, this may lower the eye pressure. Laser iridotomy reduces the risk of developing an attack of acute angle closure. In most cases, it also reduces the risk of developing chronic angle closure or of adhesions of the iris to the trabecular meshwork.</p>
<p>Diode laser cycloablation lowers IOP by reducing aqueous secretion by destroying secretory ciliary epithelium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pardianto2006_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pardianto2006-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="839" data-file-width="900" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG/220px-Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG/330px-Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG/440px-Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Conventional_surgery_to_treat_glaucoma_EDA11.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Conventional surgery to treat glaucoma makes a new opening in the <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a>, which helps fluid to leave the eye and lowers intraocular pressure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_surgery" title="Glaucoma surgery">Glaucoma surgery</a></div>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/Laser_surgery" title="Laser surgery">laser</a> and conventional surgeries are performed to treat glaucoma. Surgery is the primary therapy for those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congenital" title="Congenital">congenital</a> glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Generally, these operations are a temporary solution, as there is not yet a cure for glaucoma.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canaloplasty">Canaloplasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Canaloplasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Canaloplasty is a nonpenetrating procedure using micro<a href="/wiki/Catheter" title="Catheter">catheter</a> technology. To perform a canaloplasty, an incision is made into the eye to gain access to the <a href="/wiki/Schlemm%27s_canal" title="Schlemm's canal">Schlemm's canal</a> in a similar fashion to a viscocanalostomy. A microcatheter will circumnavigate the canal around the iris, enlarging the main drainage channel and its smaller collector channels through the injection of a sterile, gel-like material called viscoelastic. The catheter is then removed and a suture is placed within the canal and tightened.</p>
<p>By opening the canal, the pressure inside the eye may be relieved, although the reason is unclear, since the canal (of Schlemm) does not have any significant fluid resistance in glaucoma or healthy eyes. Long-term results are not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trabeculectomy">Trabeculectomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Trabeculectomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most common conventional surgery performed for glaucoma is the <a href="/wiki/Trabeculectomy" title="Trabeculectomy">trabeculectomy</a>. Here, a partial thickness flap is made in the scleral wall of the eye, and a window opening is made under the flap to remove a portion of the trabecular meshwork. The scleral flap is then sutured loosely back in place to allow fluid to flow out of the eye through this opening, resulting in lowered intraocular pressure and the formation of a bleb or fluid bubble on the surface of the eye.</p>
<p>Scarring can occur around or over the flap opening, causing it to become less effective or lose effectiveness altogether. Traditionally, chemotherapeutic adjuvants, such as <a href="/wiki/Mitomycin_C" title="Mitomycin C">mitomycin C</a> (MMC) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-fluorouracil" title="5-fluorouracil">5-fluorouracil</a> (5-FU), are applied with soaked sponges on the wound bed to prevent filtering blebs from scarring by inhibiting fibroblast proliferation. Contemporary alternatives to prevent the scarring of the meshwork opening include the sole or combinative implementation of nonchemotherapeutic adjuvants such as the ologen collagen matrix, which has been clinically shown to increase the success rates of surgical treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23640614_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23640614-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Collagen matrix prevents scarring by randomizing and modulating fibroblast proliferation in addition to mechanically preventing wound contraction and adhesion.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaucoma_drainage_implants">Glaucoma drainage implants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Glaucoma drainage implants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glaucoma_valve" title="Glaucoma valve">Glaucoma valve</a></div>
<p>Professor Anthony Molteno developed the first glaucoma drainage implant, in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a> in 1966.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Since then, several types of implants have followed on from the original, the Baerveldt tube shunt, or the valved implants, such as the Ahmed glaucoma valve implant or the ExPress Mini Shunt and the later generation pressure ridge Molteno implants. These are indicated for glaucoma patients not responding to maximal medical therapy, with previous failed guarded filtering surgery (trabeculectomy). The flow tube is inserted into the anterior chamber of the eye, and the plate is implanted underneath the conjunctiva to allow a flow of aqueous fluid out of the eye into a chamber called a <a href="/wiki/Bleb_(medicine)" title="Bleb (medicine)">bleb</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li>The first-generation Molteno and other nonvalved implants sometimes require the ligation of the tube until the bleb formed is mildly fibrosed and water-tight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VicrylTie_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-VicrylTie-67">[67]</a></sup> This is done to reduce postoperative hypotony—sudden drops in postoperative intraocular pressure.</li>
<li>Valved implants, such as the Ahmed glaucoma valve, attempt to control postoperative hypotony by using a mechanical valve.</li>
<li>Ab interno implants, such as the Xen Gel Stent, are transscleral implants by an ab interno procedure to channel aqueous humor into the non-dissected Tenon's space, creating a subconjunctival drainage area similar to a bleb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The implants are transscleral and different from more other ab interno implants that do not create a transscleral drainage, such as iStent, CyPass, or Hydrus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The ongoing scarring over the conjunctival dissipation segment of the shunt may become too thick for the aqueous humor to filter through. This may require preventive measures using antifibrotic medications, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/5-fluorouracil" title="5-fluorouracil">5-fluorouracil</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitomycin-C" title="Mitomycin-C">mitomycin-C</a> (during the procedure), or other nonantifibrotic medication methods, such as collagen matrix implant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> or biodegradable spacer, or later on create a necessity for revision surgery with the sole or combinative use of donor patch grafts or collagen matrix implant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oana_2012_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oana_2012-74">[74]</a></sup> And for glaucomatous painful blind eye and some cases of glaucoma, cyclocryotherapy for ciliary body ablation could be considered to be performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pardianto.2C_et_al.2C_2006_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pardianto.2C_et_al.2C_2006-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Laser-assisted_nonpenetrating_deep_sclerectomy">Laser-assisted nonpenetrating deep sclerectomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Laser-assisted nonpenetrating deep sclerectomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The most common surgical approach currently used for the treatment of glaucoma is <a href="/wiki/Trabeculectomy" title="Trabeculectomy">trabeculectomy</a>, in which the sclera is punctured to alleviate intraocular pressure.</p>
<p>Nonpenetrating deep sclerectomy (NPDS) surgery is a similar, but modified, procedure, in which instead of puncturing the scleral bed and <a href="/wiki/Trabecular_meshwork" title="Trabecular meshwork">trabecular meshwork</a> under a scleral flap, a second deep scleral flap is created, excised, with further procedures of deroofing the <a href="/wiki/Schlemm%27s_canal" title="Schlemm's canal">Schlemm's canal</a>, upon which, percolation of liquid from the inner eye is achieved and thus alleviating intraocular pressure, without penetrating the eye. NPDS is demonstrated to cause significantly fewer side effects than trabeculectomy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, NPDS is performed manually and requires higher level of skills that may be assisted with instruments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In order to prevent wound adhesion after deep scleral excision and to maintain good filtering results, NPDS as with other non-penetrating procedures is sometimes performed with a variety of biocompatible spacer or devices, such as the Aquaflow collagen wick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> ologen Collagen Matrix,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23640614_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23640614-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> or Xenoplast glaucoma implant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Laser-assisted NPDS is performed with the use of a CO<sub>2</sub> laser system. The laser-based system is self-terminating once the required scleral thickness and adequate drainage of the intraocular fluid have been achieved. This self-regulation effect is achieved as the CO<sub>2</sub> laser essentially stops ablating as soon as it comes in contact with the intraocular percolated liquid, which occurs as soon as the laser reaches the optimal residual intact layer thickness.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In open-angle glaucoma, the typical progression from normal vision to complete blindness takes about 25 years to 70 years without treatment, depending on the method of estimation used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heijl_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heijl-80">[80]</a></sup> The intraocular pressure can also have an effect, with higher pressures reducing the time until blindness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/250px-Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/375px-Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/500px-Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glaucoma_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for glaucoma per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>

<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff65; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> fewer than 20</div>

<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff8400; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 135–158</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff6e00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 158–181</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff5800; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 181–204</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff4200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 204–227</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff2c00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 227–250</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cb0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> more than 250</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2010, there were 44.7 million people in the world with open angle glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NumberWorldwide_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-NumberWorldwide-83">[83]</a></sup> The same year, there were 2.8 million people in the United States with open angle glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NumberWorldwide_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-NumberWorldwide-83">[83]</a></sup> By 2020, the prevalence is projected to increase to 58.6 million worldwide and 3.4 million the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NumberWorldwide_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-NumberWorldwide-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both internationally and in the United States glaucoma is the second-leading cause of blindness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Man2015_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-Man2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Globally <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a> are a more common cause. Glaucoma is also the leading cause of blindness in African Americans, who have higher rates of primary open angle glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Bilateral vision loss can negatively affect mobility and interfere with driving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analysis</a> published in 2009 found that people with primary open angle glaucoma do <i>not</i> have increased <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rates</a>, or increased risk of cardiovascular death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The association of elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) and the eye disease glaucoma was first described by Englishman <a href="/wiki/Richard_Banister" title="Richard Banister">Richard Bannister</a> in 1622: "...that the Eye be grown more solid and hard, then naturally it should be...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The invention of the ophthalmoscope by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermann_Helmholtz" title="Hermann Helmholtz">Hermann Helmholtz</a> in 1851 enabled ophthalmologists for the first time to identify the pathological hallmark of glaucoma, the excavation of the optic nerve head due to retinal ganglion cell loss. The first reliable instrument to measure intraocular pressure was invented by Norwegian ophthalmologist <a href="/wiki/Hjalmar_August_Schi%C3%B8tz" title="Hjalmar August Schiøtz">Hjalmar August Schiøtz</a> in 1905. About half a century later, Hans Goldmann in Berne, Switzerland, developed his applanation tonometer which still today - despite numerous new innovations in diagnostics - is considered the gold standard of determining this crucial pathogenic factor. In the late 20th century, further pathomechanisms beyond elevated IOP were discovered and became the subject of research like insufficient blood supply - often associated with low or irregular blood pressure - to the retina and optic nerve head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The first drug to reduce IOP, <a href="/wiki/Pilocarpine" title="Pilocarpine">pilocarpine</a>, was introduced in the 1870s. Early surgical techniques like iridectomy and fistulating methods have recently been supplemented by less invasive procedures like small implants, a range of options now widely called MIGS (micro-invasive glaucoma surgery).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word "glaucoma" comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> γλαύκωμα, a derivative of γλαυκóς, which commonly described the color of eyes which were not dark (i.e. blue, green, light gray). Eyes described as γλαυκóς due to disease might have had a gray cataract in the Hippocratic era, or, in the early Common Era, the greenish pupillary hue sometimes seen in angle-closure glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lef2015_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lef2015-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clinical_Ophthalmology_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clinical_Ophthalmology-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
