<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rho_kinase_inhibitors">Rho kinase inhibitors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Rho kinase inhibitors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rho_kinase" title="Rho kinase">Rho kinase</a> inhibitors, such as <a href="/wiki/Ripasudil" title="Ripasudil">ripasudil</a>, work by inhibition of the actin cytoskeleton, resulting in the morphological changes in the trabecular meshwork and increased aqueous outflow. More compounds in this class are being investigated in phase 2 and phase 3 trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroprotective_agents">Neuroprotective agents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Neuroprotective agents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2013 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochrane_Collaboration" title="Cochrane Collaboration">Cochrane Systematic Review</a> compared the effect of brimonidine and timolol in slowing the progression of open angle glaucoma in adult participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sena_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sena-92">[92]</a></sup> The results showed that participants assigned to brimonidine showed less visual field progression that those assigned to timolol, though the results were not significant, given the heavy <a href="/wiki/Lost_to_follow-up" title="Lost to follow-up">loss-to-followup</a> and limited evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sena_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sena-92">[92]</a></sup> The mean intraocular pressures for both groups were similar. Participants in the brimonidine group had a higher occurrence of side effects caused by medication than participants in the timolol group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sena_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sena-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cannabis">Cannabis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glaucoma&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Cannabis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Studies in the 1970s reported that the use of <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">cannabis</a> may lower intraocular pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOA_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOA-95">[95]</a></sup> In an effort to determine whether marijuana, or drugs derived from it, might be effective as a glaucoma treatment, the US <a href="/wiki/National_Eye_Institute" title="National Eye Institute">National Eye Institute</a> supported research studies from 1978 to 1984. These studies demonstrated some derivatives of marijuana lowered intraocular pressure when administered orally, intravenously, or by smoking, but not when topically applied to the eye.</p>
<p>In 2003, the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Ophthalmology" title="American Academy of Ophthalmology">American Academy of Ophthalmology</a> released a position statement stating that cannabis was not more effective than prescription medications. Furthermore, no scientific evidence has been found that demonstrates increased benefits and/or diminished risks of cannabis use to treat glaucoma compared with the wide variety of pharmaceutical agents now available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOA_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-AOA-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012 the American Glaucoma Society published a position paper discrediting the use of cannabis as a legitimate treatment for elevated intraocular pressure, for reasons including short duration of action and side effects that limit many activities of daily living.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
